reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F

class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, input_channels, stages, block_widths, output_classes):
        """
        :param input_channels: int, Number of input channels for the first layer
        :param stages: int, Number of stages in the RegNet architecture
        :param block_widths: List[int], Width (number of channels) for each block in the stages
        :param output_classes: int, Number of output classes for classification
        """
        super(Model, self).__init__()

        self.stages = stages
        self.block_widths = block_widths
        
        layers = []
        current_channels = input_channels
        
        # Construct the stages with their respective blocks
        for i in range(stages):
            layers.append(self._make_stage(current_channels, block_widths[i]))
            current_channels = block_widths[i]
        
        self.feature_extractor = nn.Sequential(*layers)
        
        # Final fully connected layer for classification
        self.fc = nn.Linear(block_widths[-1], output_classes)
    
    def _make_stage(self, in_channels, out_channels):
        """
        Creates a simple block for each stage.
        :param in_channels: int, number of input channels
        :param out_channels: int, number of output channels
        :return: nn.Sequential block with convolutional layers
        """
        return nn.Sequential(
            nn.Conv2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.BatchNorm2d(out_channels),
            nn.ReLU(),
            nn.Conv2d(out_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.BatchNorm2d(out_channels),
            nn.ReLU(),
            nn.MaxPool2d(kernel_size=2, stride=2)
        )

    def forward(self, x):
        """
        Forward pass through the RegNet model.
        :param x: torch.Tensor of shape (batch_size, input_channels, height, width)
        :return: torch.Tensor of shape (batch_size, output_classes)
        """
        x = self.feature_extractor(x)
        x = torch.mean(x, dim=[2, 3])  # Global Average Pooling
        x = self.fc(x)
        return x

# Test code for the RegNet model
batch_size = 8
input_channels = 3
image_height, image_width = 224, 224
stages = 3
block_widths = [64, 128, 256]
output_classes = 10

def get_inputs():
    """ Generates random input tensor of shape (batch_size, input_channels, height, width) """
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, input_channels, image_height, image_width)]

def get_init_inputs():
    """ Initializes model parameters """
    return [input_channels, stages, block_widths, output_classes]

Let's think about the design of the direct conv kernel. **Kernel design for Conv + BN + ReLU:** Grid: (N, C_out, H_out, W_out) can be mapped to blocks and threads. One simple mapping: each thread computes one output element (one output channel, one spatial location). This is straightforward. The thread reads input (C_in, 3, 3) and weight (C_in, 3, 3) for its output channel. Since each thread handles one output channel, it reads the weight for that channel once (or caches in registers). The input is read from global memory. Neighboring threads read overlapping input, which benefits from L2 cache. For C_in up to 256, each thread does 9*C_in multiply-adds. With 256 C_in, that's 2304 FMAs. A thread can do this in a loop. Since C_in is a run-time parameter, we can't fully unroll, but we can loop over C_in. But the issue is thread divergence? No. Memory coalescing: threads in a warp accessing the same spatial location but different output channels will read the same input pixels (good for broadcast) and different weights. Weight reads are coalesced if threads in a warp read consecutive weight values. Since each thread reads weight for its own output channel, the weight index is `c_out * C_in * 9 + ...`. Threads with consecutive c_out will read consecutive weight blocks. This is coalesced if we process output channels in groups. However, reading input: threads in a warp with different spatial locations but same batch and c_out? If each thread handles one spatial location and one channel, then within a warp, threads might have different spatial locations, leading to uncoalesced input access. Better to have a warp process a tile of spatial locations for the same output channel(s), or same spatial location for consecutive output channels. Mapping options: Option A: Each block handles a batch index n and a tile of spatial locations (TH, TW) and a group of output channels TC_out. Within the block, threads can be arranged as (TC_out, TH, TW). Let's say blockDim = (TC_out, TH, TW). For coalescing, it's best to have the fastest varying dimension correspond to contiguous memory. Output tensor layout is N, C_out, H, W. So W is contiguous, then H, then C_out. So if we have a warp cover a contiguous set of W values, memory access to output is coalesced. For input, W is also contiguous. So a good mapping is: assign each thread one (c_out, h, w). Let blockDim.x = TW, blockDim.y = TH, blockDim.z = TC_out. But warp size is 32; if TW=32, TH=1, TC_out=1, then a warp covers 32 consecutive W for one h and one c_out. That gives coalesced output writes. Input reads: each thread reads a 3x3 window around (h,w). Threads in the warp read windows that overlap heavily in W. For a given c_in, the loads will be contiguous or near-contiguous, benefiting from L2. Weight loads: all threads in the warp load weight for the same c_out, so identical loads (broadcast). That's efficient. So block configuration: `blockDim(TW, TH, TC_out)`. Choose TW=32, TH=4, TC_out=1 => 128 threads. Or TW=16, TH=8 => 128. Let's pick TW=16, TH=8 = 128 threads. Then each block computes a tile of 16x8 spatial positions for one output channel. We can have multiple blocks per output channel to cover H*W. Grid: `dim3((W+TW-1)/TW, (H+TH-1)/TH, N*C_out)`. This is straightforward. In the kernel, each block knows `n = blockIdx.z / C_out`, `c_out = blockIdx.z % C_out`. Each thread computes its (w, h) and loops over c_in and kh, kw. It accumulates in a register. Because we process one output channel per block, weight for that channel can be loaded into shared memory (C_in*9 floats). For C_in=256, that's 9,216 floats = 36.8KB. That's fine. Load weight once per block into shared memory, then all threads use it. This avoids repeated global weight loads. Similarly, we can load input tile into shared memory. For a block covering TW=16, TH=8, with kernel 3x3 and padding=1, the input tile size is (TH+2) x (TW+2). For C_in, we can load one channel at a time: for each c_in, load (TH+2)*(TW+2) input floats into shared memory (~10*18=180 floats = 720B). Then compute partial products for all threads. This is very efficient. After processing all c_in, write output. This is a very good design. Let's flesh it out. **Fused Conv + BN + ReLU kernel (eval mode):** Input: `x` (N, C_in, H, W) Weight: `w` (C_out, C_in, 3, 3) Bias: `b` (C_out) BN running_mean, running_var, weight (gamma), bias (beta), eps. Output: `y` (N, C_out, H, W) We can pass `training` flag and mean/var buffers if needed. For eval mode: after conv sum, add bias, then `y = ((sum + b) - rm) * inv_std * gamma + beta`, then ReLU. We can compute `scale = gamma * inv_std` and `shift = beta - rm * gamma * inv_std` per channel, precompute on host and pass as `bn_scale` and `bn_shift` arrays. Then in kernel: `val = sum * scale + shift` (since `sum` already includes bias? Wait: `y = ((sum + bias) - running_mean) * inv_std * gamma + beta = (sum + bias) * inv_std * gamma + (beta - running_mean * inv_std * gamma)`. So `scale = inv_std * gamma`, `shift = beta - running_mean * inv_std * gamma + bias * inv_std * gamma`. Actually we can include bias in shift: `shift = beta - running_mean * scale + bias * scale`. Then `val = sum * scale + shift`. Then ReLU: `max(val, 0)`. So precompute per channel: `scale[c]`, `shift[c]`. Then kernel: ``` __global__ void fused_conv_bn_relu_eval( const float* x, const float* w, const float* scale, const float* shift, float* y, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W) { // block per (n, c_out) int n = blockIdx.z / C_out; int c_out = blockIdx.z % C_out; int w0 = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int h0 = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; if (w0 >= W || h0 >= H) return; float sum = 0.0f; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) { for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; ++kh) { int h_in = h0 + kh - 1; if (h_in < 0 || h_in >= H) continue; // but padding is 1, input spatial dims same as output for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; ++kw) { int w_in = w0 + kw - 1; if (w_in < 0 || w_in >= W) continue; int x_idx = ((n * C_in + c_in) * H + h_in) * W + w_in; int w_idx = ((c_out * C_in + c_in) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw; sum += x[x_idx] * w[w_idx]; } } } sum = sum * scale[c_out] + shift[c_out]; sum = fmaxf(sum, 0.0f); int y_idx = ((n * C_out + c_out) * H + h0) * W + w0; y[y_idx] = sum; } ``` But this reads weights from global memory in the inner loop. To optimize, we can preload weight for the current c_out and c_in into a register or shared memory. Because each block handles one c_out, we can preload the weights for that c_out into shared memory: ``` __shared__ float w_shared[3*3*C_IN_MAX]; ``` But C_in is up to 256, so 9*256=2304 floats = 9KB. That's fine. We can allocate dynamic shared memory or static with a max. Since we need a generic kernel, we can use dynamic shared memory: `extern __shared__ float s_mem[];` and split between weights and input tile. Alternatively, we can not preload and rely on L2 cache for weights. Since all threads in a block read the same weight for a given c_in, kh, kw, the global load will be cached and broadcast. On modern GPUs, broadcasting from L2 is fast. So we might not need shared memory for weights. We can preload the input tile into shared memory to reuse across c_out? No, each thread handles one spatial pixel, so the input for that pixel is only needed by that thread. Neighboring threads need overlapping input. Loading into shared memory would allow reuse, but if the input is in global memory, L2 cache handles it. For small tiles, L1 might help. But explicit shared memory for input tile could improve performance. Simplest kernel: Each thread loops over c_in, kh, kw and loads x and w directly. This is naive but might still be okay given the small model. However, the memory access pattern for x is not coalesced across threads in the warp if each thread is reading its own h, w offset. Wait, if blockDim.x is W-dimension (contiguous), and threads in a warp have consecutive w0 and same h0, c_in, kh, kw, then the x loads are at addresses `base + w0 + kw - 1`, which are consecutive. So x loads are coalesced! Great. w loads are identical across the warp (same c_out, c_in, kh, kw). So w loads are broadcast. This is already a good memory access pattern. So we might not need shared memory at all. Let's verify: blockDim.x = 32 (W), blockDim.y = 4 (H). In a warp (32 threads), they could be along W for a fixed H (if blockDim.x >= 32). If blockDim.x = 32, blockDim.y = 4, then threads are ordered (x,y). Typically threadIdx = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y*blockDim.x + threadIdx.z*blockDim.x*blockDim.y. So the first 32 threads have threadIdx.y=0 and threadIdx.x=0..31. So they cover 32 consecutive W for H fixed. That's perfect coalescing for x loads. However, for different kh, kw, the addresses shift by `h_in*W + w_in`. For fixed kh, h_in is same for all threads in the warp. For fixed kw, the addresses are `base + w0 + kw - 1`, still consecutive. For each (c_in, kh, kw), we loop over them sequentially. So for each iteration, all threads in the warp load consecutive x values. This is fully coalesced. The w value is loaded from global memory but is constant across the warp, so L2 broadcast. Thus, the naive direct conv kernel actually has excellent memory coalescing! The compute will be limited by memory bandwidth and instruction throughput. But the inner loops might cause a lot of instructions. However, with compiler unrolling for kh and kw (since 3 is constant), the inner loop over c_in can be a simple for-loop. Given that the conv compute is small (7.4 GFLOPs), and memory bandwidth is high, this kernel might perform okay. But for stage 2 and 3, C_in is 128 or 256. Each thread does 9*256=2304 FMAs. At some throughput, a warp (32 threads) does 73k FMAs. On A100, FP32 throughput is 19.5 TFLOPS. If we have 2560 CUDA cores, each clock does 1 FMA per core. A100 clock ~1.4GHz gives ~3.5 TFLOPS? Wait, A100 has 6912 FP32 CUDA cores? Actually A100 has 6912 FP32 cores per SM? Let's recall: A100 has 64 FP32 cores per SM, 108 SMs = 6912 FP32 cores. Peak FP32 is 19.5 TFLOPS. So per cycle per core ~ 19.5e12 / (6912 * 1.41e9) = 2? Actually each core can do 2 FMA per cycle? Let's not worry. The point is 2304 FMAs is small. The bigger issue is that the kernel might be slower than cuDNN's implicit GEMM which uses Tensor Cores. But cuDNN for these tiny sizes might also be memory bound and have overhead. No. Alternatively, we can fuse Conv+BN+ReLU+Conv+BN+ReLU+MaxPool into a single kernel by writing a direct conv and doing the whole stage atomically. This would eliminate all intermediate writes. Let's consider writing this. **Stage kernel design:** Given a stage with C_in -> C_out -> C_out. We can write a kernel `stage_kernel` that processes a tile of the final output (after maxpool). Since maxpool downsamples by 2, the final output spatial size is H/2 x W/2. If each thread computes one final output pixel (for one output channel), it needs to compute the second conv's output for the corresponding 2x2 block, then max over them. But the second conv input (after first ReLU) is needed for all 4 positions. So a thread must compute 4 conv2 outputs. Each conv2 output requires 3x3xC_out reads from the first ReLU output. But the first ReLU output is the result of conv1+BN1+ReLU1. So the thread must compute conv1 outputs for the union of the 3x3 neighborhoods around the 4 positions. That union is a region of size (3+2+1) x (3+2+1)? Let's see: positions (2h, 2w), (2h, 2w+1), (2h+1, 2w), (2h+1, 2w+1). For a 3x3 kernel centered at each, the input region needed spans from 2h-1 to 2h+2 in h, and similarly in w. So 4x4 region of conv1 output? Wait, for position (2h,2w), input region is rows [2h-1, 2h+1], cols [2w-1, 2w+1]. For (2h,2w+1): rows [2h-1, 2h+1], cols [2w, 2w+2]. For (2h+1,2w): rows [2h, 2h+2], cols [2w-1,2w+1]. For (2h+1,2w+1): rows [2h,2h+2], cols [2w,2w+2]. Union is rows [2h-1, 2h+2] => 4 rows. Cols [2w-1, 2w+2] => 4 cols. So a 4x4 region of the first ReLU output is needed for each output channel. That 4x4 region has C_out channels. For C_out=64, that's 4*4*64 = 1024 floats = 4KB. That's manageable in registers/shared memory. But the 4x4 region itself is computed from the original input via conv1. To compute conv1 output for a 4x4 tile (with padding), we need a 6x6 region of the original input (since conv1 is 3x3). For C_in=3, that's 6*6*3=108 floats. For C_in=64, that's 6*6*64=2304 floats. That's also manageable. So a thread could compute a 4x4 tile of conv1 output (for all C_out channels), keep it in registers or shared memory, then compute 4 conv2 outputs (for one channel) and reduce. But a thread computing only one final output channel and one spatial location would need to process C_out channels of the 4x4 tile and C_out output channels of conv2. That's a lot. Instead, we can have a block compute a tile of final output (e.g., 8x8 spatial locations after maxpool) for a group of output channels (e.g., 8). Then the block needs to compute conv2 outputs for a 16x16 region before maxpool for those 8 channels. To compute conv2, it needs the first ReLU output for a 18x18 region for all C_out channels. That's 18*18*64 = 20736 floats for stage1, which is 83KB. That fits in shared memory! For stage2, C_out=128, 18*18*128=41472 floats = 166KB, which may exceed shared memory. For stage3, C_out=256, 18*18*256 = 82944 floats = 332KB, too big. We could use smaller tiles for later stages. Or process channels in slices. Alternatively, we can process one output channel at a time (for conv2). The block handles a tile of final spatial locations for one output channel. It needs to compute conv2 for a 16x16 pre-pool region. It needs the first ReLU output for an 18x18 region for all C_out channels (since conv2 is over all C_out). That's 18*18*C_out. For C_out=256, that's 332KB. Too big. So tiling both spatial and channel dimensions is needed. For stage3, we could slice C_out into chunks, e.g., 32 or 64. For chunk=32, 18*18*32 = 10,368 floats = 41.5KB. Plus accumulators for 16*16*1 = 256 floats. That's okay. We would accumulate conv2 output for one output channel by iterating over chunks of input channels, loading a 18x18x32 tile into shared memory, and applying weights for the output channel over that chunk. This is essentially a manualGEMM but we can do it. But this is getting complex. However, it's doable. Let's think about a simpler custom conv kernel that writes intermediate results but uses cuDNN for conv. Then fuse BN+ReLU(+MaxPool) in a custom kernel. Writing a fused kernel that avoids intermediate writes is likely the key. Let's design the custom kernel for a generic stage (conv1+bn1+relu1, conv2+bn2+relu2+maxpool). But the simplest is two separate kernels. **Kernel 1: conv3x3 + scale/shift + ReLU** We can write a templated kernel that takes input, weight, bias, scale, shift, and writes output. For simplicity and generality, let's write a Python wrapper that loops over stages and calls the kernel. The kernel can handle arbitrary N, C_in, C_out, H, W. Let's write the kernel as: ```cpp __global__ void fused_conv3x3_bn_relu_eval_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ w, const float* __restrict__ scale, const float* __restrict__ shift, float* __restrict__ y, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W) { // grid: (ceil(W / TW), ceil(H / TH), N * C_out) // block: (TW, TH, 1) int c_out = blockIdx.z % C_out; int n = blockIdx.z / C_out; int w_out = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int h_out = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; if (w_out >= W || h_out >= H) return; float sum = 0.0f; int base_x_n = n * C_in * H * W; int base_w_cout = c_out * C_in * 9; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) { int base_x_cin = base_x_n + c_in * H * W; int base_w_cin = base_w_cout + c_in * 9; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; ++kh) { int h_in = h_out + kh - 1; if (h_in < 0 || h_in >= H) continue; int row_x = base_x_cin + h_in * W; int row_w = base_w_cin + kh * 3; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; ++kw) { int w_in = w_out + kw - 1; if (w_in < 0 || w_in >= W) continue; sum += x[row_x + w_in] * w[row_w + kw]; } } } sum = sum * scale[c_out] + shift[c_out]; sum = fmaxf(sum, 0.0f); y[((n * C_out + c_out) * H + h_out) * W + w_out] = sum; } ``` This kernel loads x and w from global memory. The memory access pattern: for each (c_in, kh, kw), threads in a warp (with same h_out, contiguous w_out) read consecutive x values (row_x + w_in). This is coalesced. w loads are identical across warp. This is efficient. But there is a problem with the loop order: The inner loop over `kw` accesses x with stride 1 (good). The outer loops over `c_in` and `kh` have large strides. Since we loop over c_in (up to 256), the inner loop does 9 iterations. For each c_in, each thread does 9 loads from x and 9 loads from w. Because w is constant across warp, it stays in L1/L2. x loads are coalesced. Potential performance issue: For each thread, the loop over c_in has 256 iterations, each with some overhead. But the arithmetic intensity is moderate. We can also preload x tile into shared memory. Let's see if that helps. For a block of (TW=32, TH=8), the input tile needed is (TH+2) x (TW+2) = 10x34. For C_in=256, loading the entire tile for all c_in is too much (86KB per c_in). But we can process c_in in batches, e.g., load a 10x34 tile for 8 channels into shared memory (8*10*34=2720 floats = 10.9KB). Then compute partial sums for those 8 channels. This increases register/shared memory usage but reduces global memory reads for x because each x element is loaded once and reused by all threads in the block. However, the naive kernel already has coalesced loads and L2 cache should capture reuse across threads in a warp. But across warps (different w_out/h_out), there is less reuse. Shared memory tile ensures reuse across the entire block. For simplicity, the naive kernel might be enough. Let's try it. We also need the second kernel: conv3x3 + bn + relu + maxpool. Similar to kernel 1, but each thread computes one output pixel after maxpool. That corresponds to a 2x2 block in the pre-pool feature map. The thread must compute 4 conv outputs (for (2h,2w), (2h,2w+1), (2h+1,2w), (2h+1,2w+1)) and take the max after BN+ReLU. Kernel 2: ``` __global__ void fused_conv3x3_bn_relu_maxpool_eval_kernel(...) { int c_out = blockIdx.z % C_out; int n = blockIdx.z / C_out; int w_pool = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int h_pool = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; if (w_pool >= W/2 || h_pool >= H/2) return; float max_val = -INFINITY; for (int h_offset = 0; h_offset < 2; ++h_offset) { int h_out = h_pool * 2 + h_offset; for (int w_offset = 0; w_offset < 2; ++w_offset) { int w_out = w_pool * 2 + w_offset; // compute conv at (h_out, w_out) float sum = 0.0f; ... same conv loops ... sum = sum * scale[c_out] + shift[c_out]; sum = fmaxf(sum, 0.0f); max_val = fmaxf(max_val, sum); } } y[((n * C_out + c_out) * (H/2) + h_pool) * (W/2) + w_pool] = max_val; } ``` Wait, but the conv input to the second conv is the output of the first ReLU, which has dimensions (N, C_out, H, W). So for stage i, input to second conv is `mid` with C = out_channels, H, W. This design is straightforward. However, we need to handle cases where H or W is odd? Here they are even: 224,112,56. After maxpool, they are halved. We'll assume even for simplicity, or handle floor division. In the first kernel, `H` and `W` are the spatial dims of input and output (since padding=1, stride=1). In the second kernel, input `H` and `W` are same as output of first kernel, and output is H/2, W/2. We need to be careful about integer division: `W_out = W / 2`, `H_out = H / 2`. In C++ kernel, we can pass `H_out` and `W_out` and assume `H == H_out*2`, `W == W_out*2`. Or pass `H` and `W` and compute `h_out*2 + offset`. Now, for training mode, we need to decide what to do. We just need to make sure `var` is computed as population variance (unbiased=False). Let's verify with a quick mental test. In PyTorch: ```python bn = nn.BatchNorm2d(64) x = torch.randn(8,64,112,112) out = bn(x) mean_ref = x.mean(dim=[0,2,3]) var_ref = x.var(dim=[0,2,3], unbiased=False) scale = bn.weight / torch.sqrt(var_ref + bn.eps) shift = bn.bias - mean_ref * scale out2 = (x - mean_ref[None,:,None,None]) / torch.sqrt(var_ref[None,:,None,None] + bn.eps) * bn.weight[None,:,None,None] + bn.bias[None,:,None,None] torch.allclose(out, out2) # should be True ``` Yes, this holds. The variance used by BN is indeed the biased variance. Wait, is it always? Actually, I recall that PyTorch's BatchNorm uses the population variance (biased) for normalization during training, but the running variance uses a different formula? Let me check. PyTorch docs for nn.BatchNorm2d: "The standard-deviation is calculated via the biased estimator, equivalent to torch.var(input, unbiased=False)." So yes, normalization uses biased variance. Also, the running variance is updated with the same biased variance (with momentum). Wait, docs also say: "Also by default, during training this layer keeps running estimates of its computed mean and variance, which are then used for normalization during evaluation. The running estimates are kept with a default momentum of 0.1." In the code, the running variance is updated with the biased variance. So computing `var = x.var(dim=[0,2,3], unbiased=False)` is correct. Thus, we can compute `mean` and `var` using PyTorch, compute `scale` and `shift`, and pass to our fused kernel. The kernel does the same as eval but with per-batch scale/shift. This is great! We can use the same custom kernels for both train and eval mode. We just compute scale and shift differently: - Train: compute mean and var of conv output; scale = gamma / sqrt(var + eps); shift = beta - mean * scale; - Eval: scale = gamma / sqrt(running_var + eps); shift = beta - running_mean * scale. And we ignore updating running stats because they don't affect forward output. But there is a catch: bias. The conv layers in the reference have bias? `nn.Conv2d` default has `bias=True`. The reference doesn't set `bias=False`. So each conv has its own bias. In our fused kernel, we add the conv bias before BN. But we can also incorporate the conv bias into the shift: `shift = beta - mean * scale + bias * scale`? Wait: `y = ((conv_out + bias) - mean) * inv_std * gamma + beta` `= (conv_out + bias) * scale + (beta - mean * scale)` `= conv_out * scale + bias * scale + beta - mean * scale` `= conv_out * scale + (beta - (mean - bias) * scale)` Alternatively, keep bias separate: compute `sum += bias[c_out]` in the kernel, then `sum = sum * scale + shift`. This is simple. We also need to know the `eps` for each BN. Use `bn.eps`. Great. So our forward pass for each stage will be: ```python # stage i # conv1 conv1_out = custom_conv3x3(x, w1, b1, ...) # returns conv1 output mean1 = conv1_out.mean(dim=[0,2,3]) var1 = conv1_out.var(dim=[0,2,3], unbiased=False) scale1 = bn1_weight / torch.sqrt(var1 + bn1_eps) shift1 = bn1_bias - mean1 * scale1 relu1_out = fused_bn_relu(conv1_out, scale1, shift1) # custom kernel # conv2 conv2_out = custom_conv3x3(relu1_out, w2, b2, ...) mean2 = conv2_out.mean(dim=[0,2,3]) var2 = conv2_out.var(dim=[0,2,3], unbiased=False) scale2 = bn2_weight / torch.sqrt(var2 + bn2_eps) shift2 = bn2_bias - mean2 * scale2 x = fused_bn_relu_maxpool(conv2_out, scale2, shift2) # custom kernel ``` Wait, but now we have two custom conv kernels (conv3x3) and two custom elementwise kernels. The custom conv kernel writes conv output to global memory, which is then read by the elementwise kernel. This is similar to using cuDNN conv. If our custom conv kernel is slower than cuDNN, we lose. Alternative: We can bake the elementwise fusion into the conv kernel. That is, kernel computes conv, then applies scale and shift (provided as inputs) and ReLU, and writes the final result. This eliminates the conv output tensor entirely! Let's define the kernels: **Kernel A (fused_conv3x3_bn_relu):** Inputs: x (N, C_in, H, W), w (C_out, C_in, 3,3), bias (C_out), scale (C_out), shift (C_out). Output: y (N, C_out, H, W). Each thread does conv + bias + scale*shift + ReLU. **Kernel B (fused_conv3x3_bn_relu_maxpool):** Inputs: x (N, C_in, H, W), w (C_out, C_in, 3,3), bias (C_out), scale (C_out), shift (C_out). Output: y (N, C_out, H/2, W/2). Each thread computes conv for 4 positions, applies BN+ReLU, takes max, writes result. This means we still need to compute conv1 output? No, Kernel A writes the ReLU output directly. Kernel B reads the ReLU output and writes the maxpool output directly. The only intermediate global memory tensor is the ReLU output between stage blocks. That's unavoidable if we separate stages. But within a stage, we avoid writing the first conv output. Wait, Kernel A writes the result of first conv+bn+relu to global memory. That's needed as input to Kernel B. So per stage, we have two kernel launches and one intermediate tensor. This is good. Reference per stage: conv1 -> BN1 -> ReLU1 -> conv2 -> BN2 -> ReLU2 -> MaxPool. That's 7 ops and intermediate tensors between each. Our approach: Kernel A (fused conv1+bn1+relu1) -> Kernel B (fused conv2+bn2+relu2+maxpool). That's 2 kernels and 1 intermediate tensor (between A and B). So we drastically reduce kernel launches and memory writes. The only remaining work is computing mean and var for the conv outputs to generate scale and shift. But in our fused kernel, we don't output the conv output! How do we compute mean and var? We need the conv output values before BN. So we have a problem: to compute scale and shift, we need the raw conv output. If we fuse conv and BN, we lose the raw conv output. If the model is in eval mode, scale and shift come from running stats, no problem. We can precompute them and fuse. But if in train mode, we need the raw conv output to compute mean and var. So for eval mode, fused conv+BN is perfect. For train mode, we need the raw conv output to compute stats. Unless we compute mean and var inside the conv kernel. That is possible: each thread computes its conv sum, and we need to reduce across the grid per channel. We can do a two-pass approach: - Pass 1: Launch a kernel that computes conv sums and stores them in a temporary tensor, and also computes partial sums per block. - Pass 2: Reduce partial sums to get mean and var. - Pass 3: Launch fused kernel with scale/shift. That's complicated. Alternatively, we can compute mean and var incrementally inside the kernel using atomic adds. Since each block handles one output channel (or a subset), we can have each thread contribute to a per-channel sum and sum-of-squares in shared memory, then atomic add to global memory. But the variance requires either a two-pass or a parallel algorithm. One-pass parallel variance is tricky. But we can compute mean via atomic add. After all blocks finish, we have per-channel sums. Then we can compute mean. Then we need to compute variance: we need sum of squared deviations from mean. That requires a second pass over the conv output. So we still need to store the conv output or recompute it. If we recompute the conv output in the second pass (to compute variance), that's twice the compute. Not great. Another option: Use Welford's online algorithm per thread, reduce within block, then atomic add to global. Welford can compute mean and M2 (sum of squares of differences) in one pass. After all blocks finish, we reduce the global M2 and mean to get variance. This is a well-known parallel algorithm. It requires atomic adds for mean and M2. Since we have only up to 256 channels, that's fine. But implementing it correctly and efficiently is a bit of work, but doable. However, if eval mode is used, we can avoid all of this. To mitigate risk, we can implement both paths: if `self.training`, we compute using PyTorch's standard ops (or a less optimized custom path). If not training, we use the highly optimized fused path. These kernels are simple and safe. **Custom Kernel: bn_relu** Inputs: `x` (N, C, H, W), `scale` (C), `shift` (C). Output `y` (N, C, H, W). Each thread processes one element: `y[idx] = max(x[idx] * scale[c] + shift[c], 0)` **Custom Kernel: bn_relu_maxpool** Inputs: `x` (N, C, H, W), `scale` (C), `shift` (C). Output `y` (N, C, H/2, W/2). Each thread processes one output element (one channel, one pool loc): Compute max over 2x2 block of `(x * scale + shift)` then ReLU? Wait, ReLU is before MaxPool. The reference: BN -> ReLU -> MaxPool. So we apply BN and ReLU to each element, then take max over 2x2 block. But note: `x` is the conv output before BN. So we apply `val = x * scale + shift`, then `val = max(val, 0)`, then maxpool over 2x2. Since ReLU is monotonic, we could compute max over the 2x2 block of the raw conv output after BN, then apply ReLU to the max: `max( max_{i,j}(x_{ij} * scale + shift), 0 )`. But is that equivalent to `max( ReLU(x_{ij} * scale + shift) )`? Yes, because ReLU is monotonic increasing: max(ReLU(a), ReLU(b)) = ReLU(max(a,b)). So we can compute `max_val = max_{i,j}(x_{ij} * scale + shift)`, then `out = max(max_val, 0)`. This saves 4 ReLU operations per thread, but that's negligible. However, it might allow us to avoid computing ReLU for each of the 4 positions if the max is negative. But for simplicity, we can compute each. Even simpler: For each of the 4 positions, compute `val = x * scale + shift; val = fmaxf(val, 0); max_val = fmaxf(max_val, val)`. That's fine. Now, for the conv part, we can call `F.conv2d` in Python or C++. The overhead of Python vs C++ for these few calls is tiny. So we can keep conv in Python. Let's estimate speedup with this approach. The reduction is small overhead. Let's design this. **Detailed algorithm for one block (first conv of a stage):** 1. `conv_out = F.conv2d(input, weight, bias, padding=1)` 2. If training: - `mean = conv_out.mean(dim=[0,2,3])` - `var = conv_out.var(dim=[0,2,3], unbiased=False)` - `inv_std = 1.0 / torch.sqrt(var + eps)` - `scale = weight_bn * inv_std` (where weight_bn is gamma) - `shift = bias_bn - mean * scale` (where bias_bn is beta) Else: - `scale = weight_bn / torch.sqrt(running_var + eps)` - `shift = bias_bn - running_mean * scale` 3. Now, we need to write the custom kernels in CUDA and bind them with `load_inline`. We also have the final global average pool and FC. We can fuse GAP and FC into a single custom kernel or just use PyTorch. GAP: mean over H,W. FC: gemv. PyTorch's `torch.mean` + `F.linear` is efficient. GAP reads it (0.8MB) and writes 8x256 (8KB). Then FC reads 8x256 and writes 8x10. Very small. No need to optimize. So the plan: Use PyTorch for conv, mean/var computation, and FC. Use custom CUDA kernels for `bn_relu` and `bn_relu_maxpool`. Let's write the code. **CUDA kernels:** `bn_relu_forward`: elementwise kernel. ```cpp __global__ void bn_relu_forward_kernel(const float* x, const float* scale, const float* shift, float* y, int N, int C, int H, int W) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * C * H * W; if (idx >= total) return; int w = idx % W; int h = (idx / W) % H; int c = (idx / (W * H)) % C; int n = idx / (W * H * C); float val = x[idx]; val = val * scale[c] + shift[c]; val = fmaxf(val, 0.0f); y[idx] = val; } ``` Grid: enough blocks to cover total elements. Use block size 256 or 512. `bn_relu_maxpool_forward`: kernel that reads conv output and writes pooled output. ```cpp __global__ void bn_relu_maxpool_forward_kernel(const float* x, const float* scale, const float* shift, float* y, int N, int C, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * C * H_out * W_out; if (idx >= total) return; int w_out = idx % W_out; int h_out = (idx / W_out) % H_out; int c = (idx / (W_out * H_out)) % C; int n = idx / (W_out * H_out * C); float max_val = -INFINITY; int h_base = h_out * 2; int w_base = w_out * 2; int base_x = ((n * C + c) * H + h_base) * W + w_base; for (int i = 0; i < 2; ++i) { int h_in = h_base + i; if (h_in >= H) continue; for (int j = 0; j < 2; ++j) { int w_in = w_base + j; if (w_in >= W) continue; float val = x[((n * C + c) * H + h_in) * W + w_in]; val = val * scale[c] + shift[c]; val = fmaxf(val, 0.0f); if (val > max_val) max_val = val; } } y[idx] = max_val; } ``` But note: The maxpool in reference is `nn.MaxPool2d(kernel_size=2, stride=2)`. It works on the ReLU output. Since we fuse ReLU and maxpool, we need to apply ReLU before max. As argued, since ReLU is monotonic, `max(ReLU(x_i)) = ReLU(max(x_i))` only if we apply ReLU after BN to each element and then max. But `max(ReLU(a), ReLU(b)) = ReLU(max(a,b))`. So we can compute the max of the BN-corrected values (before ReLU) and then apply ReLU once at the end. However, if the max is negative, ReLU will clamp to zero. If we compute max before ReLU, we save operations. Let's do that: ``` float max_val = -INFINITY; for each position: float val = x * scale + shift; if (val > max_val) max_val = val; y = fmaxf(max_val, 0.0f); ``` This is correct because maxpool takes the max of ReLU outputs. Since ReLU is monotonic, the maximum over a set after ReLU equals ReLU of the maximum. So we can apply ReLU after maxpool. But wait: What if there are multiple elements and the maximum before ReLU is negative? Then after ReLU, all are zero, and max is zero. `ReLU(max_negative) = 0`. Correct. If maximum before ReLU is positive, then ReLU doesn't change it, and it's the max after ReLU. Correct. So we can do this. It saves 3 ReLU ops per thread. Now, for the first block, we also have `bn_relu`. We could also apply the same logic, but it's just elementwise. **ModelNew class:** We need to replicate parameter storage. We can create the same `nn.Conv2d` and `nn.BatchNorm2d` and `nn.Linear` layers, so that state dict copying works. In `__init__`, we create them and move them to CUDA? Actually the model may be moved to CUDA by the harness or we can handle it in forward. Then in `forward`, we iterate over stages. For each stage, we have two convs and two BNs. Let's define ModelNew to have `self.stages` as a list of tuples: `(conv1, bn1, conv2, bn2)`. Or we can keep `nn.Sequential` for compatibility but not use it in forward. For state dict compatibility, the names must match. The reference uses: - `self.feature_extractor` (nn.Sequential) - `self.fc` (nn.Linear) Inside `self.feature_extractor`, stages are indexed 0,1,2, and each stage is a `nn.Sequential` with layers `[0]=Conv2d, [1]=BN, [2]=ReLU, [3]=Conv2d, [4]=BN, [5]=ReLU, [6]=MaxPool2d`. To keep names like `feature_extractor.0.0.weight` etc., we can instantiate the same structure. So in ModelNew `__init__`, we can do: ```python layers = [] current_channels = input_channels for i in range(stages): out_channels = block_widths[i] layers.append(nn.Sequential( nn.Conv2d(current_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=3, padding=1), nn.BatchNorm2d(out_channels), nn.ReLU(), nn.Conv2d(out_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=3, padding=1), nn.BatchNorm2d(out_channels), nn.ReLU(), nn.MaxPool2d(kernel_size=2, stride=2) )) current_channels = out_channels self.feature_extractor = nn.Sequential(*layers) self.fc = nn.Linear(block_widths[-1], output_classes) ``` This ensures state dict names match. Then in `forward`, we will iterate over `self.feature_extractor`: ```python x = input for i, stage in enumerate(self.feature_extractor): conv1 = stage[0] bn1 = stage[1] conv2 = stage[3] bn2 = stage[4] # block 1 out1 = F.conv2d(x, conv1.weight, conv1.bias, padding=1) if self.training: mean1 = out1.mean(dim=[0,2,3]) var1 = out1.var(dim=[0,2,3], unbiased=False) inv_std1 = 1.0 / torch.sqrt(var1 + bn1.eps) scale1 = bn1.weight * inv_std1 shift1 = bn1.bias - mean1 * scale1 else: inv_std1 = 1.0 / torch.sqrt(bn1.running_var + bn1.eps) scale1 = bn1.weight * inv_std1 shift1 = bn1.bias - bn1.running_mean * inv_std1 * bn1.weight # wait, we can simplify # Actually scale = weight / sqrt(var+eps), shift = bias - mean * scale. So: # shift1 = bn1.bias - (bn1.running_mean if not training else mean1) * scale1 # We can compute this uniformly. x = bn_relu_forward(out1, scale1, shift1) # custom kernel # Wait, the first custom kernel should write to a new tensor. # We can reuse a buffer. # block 2 out2 = F.conv2d(x, conv2.weight, conv2.bias, padding=1) # compute scale2, shift2 similarly x = bn_relu_maxpool_forward(out2, scale2, shift2) ``` This is straightforward. But we need to implement `bn_relu_forward` and `bn_relu_maxpool_forward` as CUDA kernels via `load_inline`. One concern: In training mode, `out1.mean(dim=[0,2,3])` returns a tensor on the same device. This is fine. Another concern: The custom kernels must handle arbitrary N, C, H, W. Yes. Performance: We have 2 cuDNN convs per stage. Fused kernels are simple elementwise. The speedup comes from reducing memory traffic for BN, ReLU, MaxPool. Should be faster than reference. But is `F.conv2d` in Python slower than `conv1(x)`? Not significantly. `conv1(x)` also calls `F.conv2d` internally. So it's the same. However, there is still a memory write for `out1` (conv1 output) and a memory read for the fused kernel. In the reference, conv1 output is written and then read by BN. The difference is that reference BN writes another tensor, which is then read by ReLU, etc. We save those extra writes/reads. For the first block, reference memory traffic after conv1: write conv1_out, read conv1_out (BN), write bn1_out, read bn1_out (ReLU), write relu1_out. That's 3 writes + 2 reads. Our path: write conv1_out, read conv1_out (fused), write relu1_out. That's 2 writes + 1 read. So we save 1 write + 1 read. For 25MB tensor, save 50MB. For 3 stages, total saved maybe 150MB+. For the second block, reference: write conv2_out, read conv2_out (BN), write bn2_out, read bn2_out (ReLU), write relu2_out, read relu2_out (MaxPool), write pool_out. That's 4 writes + 3 reads. Our path: write conv2_out, read conv2_out (fused), write pool_out. That's 2 writes + 1 read. Save 2 writes + 2 reads. For stage1 second block (102MB tensor), save 408MB. This is huge. So overall speedup could be 50% or more. Let's implement this. **Implementation details:** We need to write a Python module that defines `ModelNew` and loads the CUDA kernels via `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline`. C++ code: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void bn_relu_kernel(const float* x, const float* scale, const float* shift, float* y, int N, int C, int H, int W) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * C * H * W; if (idx >= total) return; int c = (idx / (H * W)) % C; float val = x[idx]; val = val * scale[c] + shift[c]; val = fmaxf(val, 0.0f); y[idx] = val; } __global__ void bn_relu_maxpool_kernel(const float* x, const float* scale, const float* shift, float* y, int N, int C, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * C * H_out * W_out; if (idx >= total) return; int w_out = idx % W_out; int h_out = (idx / W_out) % H_out; int c = (idx / (W_out * H_out)) % C; int n = idx / (W_out * H_out * C); int h_base = h_out * 2; int w_base = w_out * 2; float max_val = -INFINITY; for (int i = 0; i < 2; ++i) { int h_in = h_base + i; if (h_in >= H) continue; for (int j = 0; j < 2; ++j) { int w_in = w_base + j; if (w_in >= W) continue; int x_idx = ((n * C + c) * H + h_in) * W + w_in; float val = x[x_idx] * scale[c] + shift[c]; if (val > max_val) max_val = val; } } y[idx] = fmaxf(max_val, 0.0f); } torch::Tensor bn_relu_forward(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor scale, torch::Tensor shift) { int N = x.size(0); int C = x.size(1); int H = x.size(2); int W = x.size(3); auto y = torch::empty_like(x); int total = N * C * H * W; const int threads = 256; const int blocks = (total + threads - 1) / threads; bn_relu_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>(x.data_ptr<float>(), scale.data_ptr<float>(), shift.data_ptr<float>(), y.data_ptr<float>(), N, C, H, W); return y; } torch::Tensor bn_relu_maxpool_forward(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor scale, torch::Tensor shift) { int N = x.size(0); int C = x.size(1); int H = x.size(2); int W = x.size(3); int H_out = H / 2; int W_out = W / 2; auto y = torch::empty({N, C, H_out, W_out}, x.options()); int total = N * C * H_out * W_out; const int threads = 256; const int blocks = (total + threads - 1) / threads; bn_relu_maxpool_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>(x.data_ptr<float>(), scale.data_ptr<float>(), shift.data_ptr<float>(), y.data_ptr<float>(), N, C, H, W, H_out, W_out); return y; } ``` We need to make sure `x` is contiguous float tensor. We can add checks or just assume. In forward, after conv, the output is contiguous float. We also need to handle training mode update of buffers? Not needed. But we could add `momentum` updates to running_mean/var for completeness. Since output correctness doesn't depend on it for a single pass, we can skip it. For eval: ```python inv_std = 1.0 / torch.sqrt(bn.running_var + bn.eps) scale = bn.weight * inv_std shift = bn.bias - bn.running_mean * scale ``` For train: ```python mean = x.mean(dim=[0,2,3]) var = x.var(dim=[0,2,3], unbiased=False) inv_std = 1.0 / torch.sqrt(var + bn.eps) scale = bn.weight * inv_std shift = bn.bias - mean * scale ``` Note: `bn.weight` is gamma, `bn.bias` is beta. The `load_inline` call: ```python from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cpp_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void bn_relu_kernel(...) { ... } __global__ void bn_relu_maxpool_kernel(...) { ... } torch::Tensor bn_relu_forward(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor scale, torch::Tensor shift) { ... return y; } torch::Tensor bn_relu_maxpool_forward(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor scale, torch::Tensor shift) { ... return y; } """ model_new_cuda = load_inline( name="model_new_cuda", cpp_sources=cpp_source, functions=["bn_relu_forward", "bn_relu_maxpool_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3"], verbose=False, ) ``` Then call `model_new_cuda.bn_relu_forward(...)` and `model_new_cuda.bn_relu_maxpool_forward(...)`. **CUDA launch config:** For `bn_relu_forward`, total elements = N*C*H*W. For max element shape, e.g., 8*64*224*224 = 25,690,112. With block size 256, blocks = 100,352. That's okay. For `bn_relu_maxpool_forward`, total = N*C*H/2*W/2. We can just call the kernels. **Potential numerical issue:** The `bn_relu_maxpool_forward` kernel: we compute `max_val = -INFINITY`, then for each of 4 elements, compute `val = x * scale + shift`, take max. Then `y = fmaxf(max_val, 0.0f)`. If all 4 elements are -inf (shouldn't happen), max_val remains -inf. But fmaxf(-inf, 0) = 0? Actually `fmaxf(-inf, 0) = 0`. So fine. **Potential performance:** The kernel does 4 loads per thread, 4 multiplies, and some comparisons. With block size 256 and many blocks, it should saturate memory bandwidth. But there's an optimization: The `scale` and `shift` are loaded from global memory for each thread. Since threads in a block have the same c (if we map c to blockIdx?), but in our 1D indexing, threads in a warp have consecutive `idx`, which span across n, c, h, w. That means consecutive threads may have different `c`. So loading `scale[c]` is not broadcast across the warp; it may be random or strided. If we map memory such that `c` changes slowly (i.e., innermost dims are h,w), then threads in a warp have same n and c but different h,w (consecutive). Then `scale[c]` is constant across the warp, so it's loaded once and broadcast. This is good because our indexing: ``` int w_out = idx % W_out; int h_out = (idx / W_out) % H_out; int c = (idx / (W_out * H_out)) % C; int n = idx / (W_out * H_out * C); ``` The fastest-changing dimension is `w_out` (contiguous in memory). The next is `h_out`. So `c` changes every `W_out*H_out` elements. For a warp of 32 threads, if `W_out*H_out` is large (e.g., 112*112=12544), then all 32 threads in a warp will have the same `c` and `n`. This is great! `scale[c]` is broadcast. For smaller sizes (e.g., H_out=28, W_out=28 => 784), still fine. So memory access to `scale` and `shift` is very efficient. Similarly for `bn_relu_forward`, `c` changes every `H*W` elements. **Implementation of `bn_relu_forward` kernel:** Similar. The indexing ensures `scale[c]` is broadcast within warp. One small optimization: For `bn_relu_forward`, the output is `y`. We can write directly. For `bn_relu_maxpool_forward`, output is smaller. **Backward compatibility with `state_dict`:** Since `ModelNew` uses the same layer names as `Model`, copying `state_dict` should work seamlessly. **Let's write the code now.** ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cpp_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cmath> __global__ void bn_relu_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ scale, const float* __restrict__ shift, float* __restrict__ y, int N, int C, int H, int W) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * C * H * W; if (idx >= total) return; int c = (idx / (H * W)) % C; float v = x[idx]; v = v * scale[c] + shift[c]; v = fmaxf(v, 0.0f); y[idx] = v; } __global__ void bn_relu_maxpool_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ scale, const float* __restrict__ shift, float* __restrict__ y, int N, int C, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * C * H_out * W_out; if (idx >= total) return; int w_out = idx % W_out; int h_out = (idx / W_out) % H_out; int c = (idx / (W_out * H_out)) % C; int n = idx / (W_out * H_out * C); int h_base = h_out * 2; int w_base = w_out * 2; float max_val = -INFINITY; for (int i = 0; i < 2; ++i) { int h_in = h_base + i; if (h_in >= H) continue; for (int j = 0; j < 2; ++j) { int w_in = w_base + j; if (w_in >= W) continue; int x_idx = ((n * C + c) * H + h_in) * W + w_in; float v = x[x_idx] * scale[c] + shift[c]; if (v > max_val) max_val = v; } } y[idx] = fmaxf(max_val, 0.0f); } torch::Tensor bn_relu_forward(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor scale, torch::Tensor shift) { int N = x.size(0); int C = x.size(1); int H = x.size(2); int W = x.size(3); auto y = torch::empty_like(x); int total = N * C * H * W; const int threads = 256; const int blocks = (total + threads - 1) / threads; bn_relu_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), scale.data_ptr<float>(), shift.data_ptr<float>(), y.data_ptr<float>(), N, C, H, W); return y; } torch::Tensor bn_relu_maxpool_forward(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor scale, torch::Tensor shift) { int N = x.size(0); int C = x.size(1); int H = x.size(2); int W = x.size(3); int H_out = H / 2; int W_out = W / 2; auto y = torch::empty({N, C, H_out, W_out}, x.options()); int total = N * C * H_out * W_out; const int threads = 256; const int blocks = (total + threads - 1) / threads; bn_relu_maxpool_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), scale.data_ptr<float>(), shift.data_ptr<float>(), y.data_ptr<float>(), N, C, H, W, H_out, W_out); return y; } ''' cuda_module = load_inline( name='model_new_cuda', cpp_sources=cpp_source, functions=['bn_relu_forward', 'bn_relu_maxpool_forward'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3'], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, input_channels, stages, block_widths, output_classes): super().__init__() layers = [] current_channels = input_channels for i in range(stages): out_channels = block_widths[i] layers.append(nn.Sequential( nn.Conv2d(current_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=3, padding=1, bias=True), nn.BatchNorm2d(out_channels), nn.ReLU(), nn.Conv2d(out_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=3, padding=1, bias=True), nn.BatchNorm2d(out_channels), nn.ReLU(), nn.MaxPool2d(kernel_size=2, stride=2) )) current_channels = out_channels self.feature_extractor = nn.Sequential(*layers) self.fc = nn.Linear(block_widths[-1], output_classes) def _bn_params(self, bn, x): if self.training: var, mean = torch.var_mean(x, dim=[0, 2, 3], unbiased=False) else: mean = bn.running_mean var = bn.running_var inv_std = torch.rsqrt(var + bn.eps) scale = bn.weight * inv_std shift = bn.bias - mean * scale return scale, shift def forward(self, x): for stage in self.feature_extractor: conv1 = stage[0] bn1 = stage[1] conv2 = stage[3] bn2 = stage[4] out1 = F.conv2d(x, conv1.weight, conv1.bias, padding=1) scale1, shift1 = self._bn_params(bn1, out1) x = cuda_module.bn_relu_forward(out1, scale1, shift1) out2 = F.conv2d(x, conv2.weight, conv2.bias, padding=1) scale2, shift2 = self._bn_params(bn2, out2) x = cuda_module.bn_relu_maxpool_forward(out2, scale2, shift2) x = x.mean(dim=[2, 3]) x = F.linear(x, self.fc.weight, self.fc.bias) return x ``` But we need to ensure `bn_relu_forward` and `bn_relu_maxpool_forward` are accessible. The `load_inline` returns a module. We store it in a global variable. Wait, `load_inline` at module load time. The first compilation might take time but that's fine. One issue: In C++ code, we used `torch::empty` and `torch::empty_like`. These are fine. Another issue: The `cpp_sources` string should be valid C++14/17. The raw string literal `r'...'` may have backslashes? No. Should be fine. Potential issue with `torch.rsqrt`: this is a PyTorch op, fine. Potential issue: `bn.running_mean` and `bn.running_var` are buffers. Accessing them is fine. Potential issue: `bn.weight` and `bn.bias` are parameters. Accessing them is fine. Potential issue: When `self.training=True`, we compute `torch.var_mean`. This returns tensors on the same device as `out1`. Good. Potential issue: The custom kernels don't handle half precision. The task says fp32 tolerance. Inputs are fp32. Potential issue: `bn_relu_maxpool_forward` handles odd sizes by skipping out-of-bounds reads. That's fine. Now, let's think about correctness of `_bn_params`. In `nn.BatchNorm2d`, `bn.weight` is gamma, `bn.bias` is beta. So: `y = (x - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) * gamma + beta` `scale = gamma / sqrt(var + eps)` `shift = beta - mean * gamma / sqrt(var + eps) = beta - mean * scale` Yes. For `bn_relu_maxpool_forward`, we apply `x * scale + shift` to each element before taking max and then ReLU. This is correct because: `ReLU(BN(x)) = max((x - mean)/std * gamma + beta, 0) = max(x * scale + shift, 0)` And `maxpool(ReLU(BN(x))) = max_{i in pool}( max(x_i * scale + shift, 0) )` Since `max()` is monotonic and `max(a,0)` is monotonic for a, we have: `max_{i}( max(v_i, 0) ) = max( max_i(v_i), 0 )`. So we can compute `max_val = max_i(x_i * scale + shift)` and then `output = max(max_val, 0)`. Our kernel does exactly that. Yes, we compute `max_val` as the max of the transformed values before ReLU, then apply `fmaxf(max_val, 0)`. This is correct. One more nuance: The reference uses `nn.ReLU()` which, by default, operates in-place? No, `nn.ReLU()` returns a new tensor (out-of-place). So the intermediate ReLU outputs exist in memory. Our fused kernel skips creating them. That's correct. Now, let's think about potential performance bottlenecks in our Python code: We call `F.conv2d` explicitly. This avoids nn.Module overhead but is similar. We have a Python loop over stages (3 iterations). That's fine. We compute `torch.var_mean` in Python. For large tensors, this is a reduction kernel. Another potential issue: `fmaxf` requires `<cmath>` or `<cuda_runtime.h>`. `cuda_runtime.h` provides it, but including `<cmath>` is safe. Now, let's consider if we can further optimize by fusing the first block's conv into the kernel as well. That would avoid writing `out1`. But in train mode, we need `out1` for mean/var. Unless we compute mean/var inside the kernel. For eval mode, we could fuse conv. But to keep code simple and safe, we stick with this. But wait, we can add a separate eval-only path that fuses conv+bn+relu using a direct conv kernel. This could give an extra speedup if the test is in eval mode. And for train mode, we fall back to the cuDNN+fusion path. This way we maximize performance in both cases. But writing a direct conv kernel is work. However, maybe we can write a simple one and see. Actually, for eval mode, we can precompute fused conv weights: `w_fused = w * scale.view(-1,1,1,1)`, `b_fused = b + shift`. Then `conv(x, w_fused) + b_fused` is equivalent to `conv(x,w) + b` scaled and shifted? Wait: `conv(x, w_fused) + b_fused = conv(x, w * scale) + (b * scale + shift)`. Since convolution is linear, `conv(x, w * scale) = conv(x, w) * scale` (if scale is per-output-channel). So `conv(x, w_fused) + b_fused = conv(x,w)*scale + b*scale + shift = (conv(x,w) + b) * scale + shift`. This is exactly what we want! So for eval mode, we can precompute `w_fused = w * scale.view(-1,1,1,1)` and `b_fused = b * scale + shift`. Then the first block becomes just `F.conv2d(x, w_fused, b_fused, padding=1)` followed by ReLU. But we still need a ReLU kernel (or `F.relu_`). Actually `F.relu_` on the conv output in-place would be one memory pass. But we can also fuse ReLU into the conv by using a custom conv kernel. But `F.conv2d` cannot fuse ReLU directly. Wait, but we can write a custom kernel that takes `x`, `w_fused`, `b_fused`, computes conv, and then applies ReLU in-place or writes output. Since eval mode uses running stats, `scale` and `shift` are constant. We can precompute `w_fused` and `b_fused` in `forward`. This is cheap. Then we can avoid writing a custom conv kernel by using `F.conv2d` + `F.relu_`. But `F.conv2d` with `w_fused` still writes to a new tensor. Then `F.relu_` does an in-place pass. That's 1 write + 1 read-modify-write. Our custom fused kernel (conv+bn+relu) would do 1 write. But conv via cuDNN is likely faster than our custom conv. So using cuDNN conv + `F.relu_` might be better than custom conv. Similarly, for the second block in eval mode, we can precompute `w_fused` and `b_fused`. Then do `F.conv2d(x, w_fused, b_fused, padding=1)` and then our custom maxpool-after-ReLU kernel. But wait, if we pre-fuse BN into conv, then the conv output is already normalized and biased. We just need to ReLU and MaxPool. We can write a kernel that takes the conv output and does ReLU+MaxPool. That's even simpler! And we save the BN entirely. So for eval mode, we can do: - Compute `w_fused = conv.weight * scale.view(-1,1,1,1)`, `b_fused = conv.bias * scale + shift`. - `out = F.conv2d(x, w_fused, b_fused, padding=1)` - Apply `relu_maxpool_forward(out)` custom kernel (or `F.relu_` + `F.max_pool2d`). This eliminates all BN computation and the corresponding memory traffic. For train mode, we cannot fuse BN into conv because scale/shift change per batch. So we keep the previous path. Could we write a `relu_maxpool_forward` kernel? Yes, simple: ```cpp __global__ void relu_maxpool_kernel(const float* x, float* y, int N, int C, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * C * H_out * W_out; if (idx >= total) return; // ... max_val = -INFINITY; for i,j: float v = x[x_idx]; if (v > max_val) max_val = v; y[idx] = fmaxf(max_val, 0.0f); } ``` This is even faster because no scale/shift multiplication. But wait, for train mode we still need scale/shift. For eval mode, we can have an even faster path. Let's implement both paths. **Eval path optimizations:** In eval mode, for each conv+BN block, we can fuse the BN affine parameters into conv weight and bias. This is a standard inference optimization. Then the network becomes just conv + ReLU + conv + ReLU + MaxPool. Specifically: - For block 1: compute `scale1, shift1` from running stats. Compute `w1_fused = conv1.weight * scale1.view(-1,1,1,1)`, `b1_fused = conv1.bias * scale1 + shift1`. Then `out1 = F.conv2d(x, w1_fused, b1_fused, padding=1)`. Then apply ReLU (custom kernel or `F.relu_`). - For block 2: similarly `w2_fused`, `b2_fused`. `out2 = F.conv2d(out1, w2_fused, b2_fused, padding=1)`. Then apply ReLU + MaxPool (custom kernel). But note: `F.relu_` in-place modifies the tensor. The conv output is a new tensor, so in-place is safe. `F.relu_` is a single elementwise kernel (read-modify-write). Our custom `relu_forward` kernel is also read-modify-write? Actually `F.relu_` reads and writes the same memory. That's fine. But we can also write a custom `relu_forward` kernel that does the same. But `F.relu_` is already very optimized. Using it is fine. Similarly, for ReLU+MaxPool, we can write a custom kernel or just call `F.max_pool2d(F.relu_(out2), ...)`. But that does two passes: ReLU (read-modify-write) and MaxPool (read-write). Our custom kernel reads conv2_out and writes pool_out in one pass. So custom is better. Thus eval path per stage: 1. `out = F.conv2d(x, w1_fused, b1_fused, padding=1)` 2. `F.relu_(out)` # in-place 3. `out = F.conv2d(out, w2_fused, b2_fused, padding=1)` 4. `pool = relu_maxpool_forward(out)` # custom kernel This is extremely simple and efficient. Memory traffic: conv1 writes out1, relu modifies in-place (no extra memory), conv2 writes out2, custom kernel reads out2 writes pool. So only two full tensor writes per stage. Reference has many more. Train path per stage: 1. `out = F.conv2d(x, w1, b1, padding=1)` 2. compute scale1, shift1 from batch stats 3. `x = bn_relu_forward(out, scale1, shift1)` # custom kernel 4. `out = F.conv2d(x, w2, b2, padding=1)` 5. compute scale2, shift2 6. `x = bn_relu_maxpool_forward(out, scale2, shift2)` # custom kernel This is also good. Since we don't know the mode, we implement both. But there's a catch: In eval mode, fusing BN into conv changes the effective weights. If we compute `w_fused` in `forward` using the current running stats, and running stats are fixed, it's fine. But we need to be careful: if the model is on GPU, the computation of `w_fused` should be on GPU. We can do it in `forward` with simple elementwise ops. It's O(C_out*C_in*9 + C_out) which is tiny. Let's implement it. **Swapping between paths:** ```python if self.training: # train path else: # eval path, pre-fuse BN into conv ``` However, computing `w_fused` and `b_fused` for each block on every forward might add a small overhead. We can compute them once and cache, but since state dict might be loaded later, we'd need to invalidate cache. Simplest: compute on the fly. Overhead is tiny. Let's write the eval path code: ```python # For block 1 inv_std = torch.rsqrt(bn1.running_var + bn1.eps) scale1 = bn1.weight * inv_std shift1 = bn1.bias - bn1.running_mean * scale1 w1 = conv1.weight * scale1.view(-1, 1, 1, 1) b1 = conv1.bias * scale1 + shift1 x = F.conv2d(x, w1, b1, padding=1) F.relu_(x) # For block 2 inv_std = torch.rsqrt(bn2.running_var + bn2.eps) scale2 = bn2.weight * inv_std shift2 = bn2.bias - bn2.running_mean * scale2 w2 = conv2.weight * scale2.view(-1, 1, 1, 1) b2 = conv2.bias * scale2 + shift2 out = F.conv2d(x, w2, b2, padding=1) x = relu_maxpool_forward(out) ``` We need to write `relu_maxpool_forward` kernel. That's easy. But wait: If we pre-fuse BN into conv, the conv output is `conv(x, w_fused) + b_fused`. Since `w_fused = w * scale` and `b_fused = b * scale + shift`, we need to ensure shape broadcasting: `conv1.weight` shape is (C_out, C_in, 3, 3). `scale1` shape is (C_out,). So `conv1.weight * scale1.view(-1,1,1,1)` works. `conv1.bias` shape is (C_out,). `conv1.bias * scale1 + shift1` works. Then after conv, we apply ReLU. `F.relu_` modifies in-place. Then second conv. For the second block, we need ReLU then MaxPool. We can write a custom kernel `relu_maxpool_forward` that reads the conv2 output and writes pool output after ReLU+maxpool. Let's write it. ```cpp __global__ void relu_maxpool_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ y, int N, int C, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * C * H_out * W_out; if (idx >= total) return; int w_out = idx % W_out; int h_out = (idx / W_out) % H_out; int c = (idx / (W_out * H_out)) % C; int n = idx / (W_out * H_out * C); int h_base = h_out * 2; int w_base = w_out * 2; float max_val = -INFINITY; for (int i = 0; i < 2; ++i) { int h_in = h_base + i; if (h_in >= H) continue; for (int j = 0; j < 2; ++j) { int w_in = w_base + j; if (w_in >= W) continue; int x_idx = ((n * C + c) * H + h_in) * W + w_in; float v = x[x_idx]; if (v > max_val) max_val = v; } } y[idx] = fmaxf(max_val, 0.0f); } ``` This is even faster than `bn_relu_maxpool_kernel` because no scale/shift. But in eval mode, we could also just call PyTorch's `F.relu_(out)` and then `F.max_pool2d`. However, `F.max_pool2d` reads the ReLU output. Our custom kernel reads conv2 output and writes pool output directly. Since ReLU is monotonic, `maxpool(relu(x)) = relu(maxpool(x))`? Wait, that's not true in general. MaxPool takes the max over spatial locations. If we apply ReLU before MaxPool, the max is over ReLU-activated values. If we apply ReLU after MaxPool, it's `ReLU( MaxPool(x) )`. Since MaxPool selects the maximum value in a window, and ReLU is monotonic, `ReLU( max_i x_i ) = max_i ReLU(x_i)`. So yes, `MaxPool(ReLU(x)) = ReLU(MaxPool(x))`. So we could call `F.max_pool2d(out, kernel_size=2, stride=2)` and then `F.relu_` in-place on the pool output. That would be: MaxPool reads out (102MB) writes pool (25.6MB), then ReLU reads pool (25.6MB) writes in-place. Total: 1 read of large tensor + 1 write of small tensor + 1 small read/write. That's very efficient! Actually, PyTorch might even fuse ReLU into maxpool if we use `F.max_pool2d`? No. But `F.relu_(pool_out)` is a tiny pass. Wait, `F.max_pool2d(F.relu_(out), ...)` would do ReLU in-place on out (read-modify-write of 102MB), then MaxPool reads out (102MB) writes pool (25.6MB). Total: 2 reads + 1 write of large tensor + 1 write of small. Our custom kernel reads out (102MB) and writes pool (25.6MB) in one pass. So custom kernel saves the ReLU read-modify-write of 102MB. That's a modest saving (0.1ms). Still, custom kernel is better. But even with PyTorch ops, eval mode path is already quite fast: fused conv+BN via weight scaling, then `F.relu_`, then conv+BN fused, then `relu_maxpool_forward` custom kernel. The first ReLU is in-place, so it's just one kernel. No extra memory write. So eval path is very good. For train path, we need `bn_relu_forward` and `bn_relu_maxpool_forward` custom kernels. So overall, we need 3 custom kernels: - `bn_relu_forward` (train path, block 1) - `bn_relu_maxpool_forward` (train path, block 2) - `relu_maxpool_forward` (eval path, block 2) We can keep the eval path block 1 ReLU as `F.relu_`. Alternatively, we could also write a `relu_forward` custom kernel for uniformity, but `F.relu_` is fine. Wait, but in train path, we also need `bn_relu_forward`. Could we just compute `out = out * scale.view(1,-1,1,1) + shift.view(1,-1,1,1)` using PyTorch, then `F.relu_` in-place? That would be an elementwise addition/multiplication kernel (broadcasted) followed by ReLU in-place. PyTorch likely fuses the multiplication and addition into a single kernel? Actually `out * scale + shift` is a single `addcmul` or `mul+add` kernel. Then `F.relu_` is another kernel. That's 2 kernels and 2 memory passes. Our custom `bn_relu_forward` does it in 1 kernel and 1 pass. So our custom kernel saves one memory pass. Similarly, for eval path block 1: we do fused conv+bn, then `F.relu_`. The `F.relu_` is 1 pass. Compared to reference which had BN then ReLU (2 passes), we saved the BN pass (fused into conv weight). Actually we replaced conv+BN with conv using fused weights. That's a huge win. The ReLU is still a separate pass. If we wrote a custom conv+relu kernel, we could avoid the ReLU pass too. But conv is done by cuDNN, which is optimal. Adding ReLU fusion to cuDNN is not possible from Python. So `F.relu_` is acceptable. For eval path block 2: fused conv+bn, then ReLU+MaxPool via custom kernel. This saves ReLU pass and MaxPool read of large tensor. Very good. For train path block 2: conv, then custom BN+ReLU+MaxPool. This saves BN write, ReLU read/write, MaxPool read of large tensor. Very good. So overall, this is a strong solution. Now, let's think about compiling and correctness. One possible issue: The `load_inline` function might compile on first import. We need to make sure the C++ code compiles. Also, in the C++ code, `torch::empty` may require `at::kFloat` or we can use `x.options()`. `auto y = torch::empty_like(x)` is fine. For maxpool, `auto y = torch::empty({N, C, H_out, W_out}, x.options())` is fine. Let's draft the complete code. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline # C++ source cpp_source = r''' #include But if the test runs on GPU, the fallback path is never taken. I'll add the CPU fallback just in case. Also, we need to make sure that the `nn.Sequential` stages actually exist and contain the original modules. In the fallback, we can call `self.feature_extractor(x)` directly. Let's refine the code. ```python def forward(self, x): if not x.is_cuda: x = self.feature_extractor(x) x = x.mean(dim=[2, 3]) x = self.fc(x) return x for stage in self.feature_extractor: conv1 = stage[0] bn1 = stage[1] conv2 = stage[3] bn2 = stage[4] if self.training: out1 = F.conv2d(x, conv1.weight, conv1.bias, padding=1) scale1, shift1 = self._bn_scale_shift(bn1, out1) x = _cuda.bn_relu_forward(out1, scale1, shift1) else: scale1, shift1 = self._bn_scale_shift(bn1, None) w1 = conv1.weight * scale1.view(-1, 1, 1, 1) b1 = conv1.bias * scale1 + shift1 x = F.conv2d(x, w1, b1, padding=1) F.relu_(x) if self.training: out2 = F.conv2d(x, conv2.weight, conv2.bias, padding=1) scale2, shift2 = self._bn_scale_shift(bn2, out2) x = _cuda.bn_relu_maxpool_forward(out2, scale2, shift2) else: scale2, shift2 = self._bn_scale_shift(bn2, None) w2 = conv2.weight * scale2.view(-1, 1, 1, 1) b2 = conv2.bias * scale2 + shift2 out2 = F.conv2d(x, w2, b2, padding=1) x = _cuda.relu_maxpool_forward(out2) x = x.mean(dim=[2, 3]) x = F.linear(x, self.fc.weight, self.fc.bias) return x ``` This looks solid. One more thought: In eval mode, calling `F.relu_(x)` modifies `x` in-place. The variable `x` is a new tensor from `F.conv2d`, so it's safe. However, we need to be careful about autograd graph. In eval mode with `torch.no_grad()`, there is no graph. Even if there is, in-place operations on leaf variables are okay if no gradient is needed. Since we're in eval mode, no gradient. The reference also uses `nn.ReLU()` which could be in-place or not; but for inference without grad, it's fine. Potential numerical issue: When we fuse BN into conv weights, the order of operations might introduce slight floating point differences compared to separate conv and BN. Let's analyze: Reference eval: 1. `conv_out = conv(x, w) + b` 2. `bn_out = (conv_out - running_mean) / sqrt(running_var + eps) * gamma + beta` Our eval: 1. `w_fused = w * scale`, `b_fused = b * scale + shift`, where `scale = gamma / sqrt(var+eps)`, `shift = beta - running_mean * scale`. 2. `out = conv(x, w_fused) + b_fused` 3. `out = ReLU(out)` Since convolution is linear: `conv(x, w_fused) = conv(x, w * scale) = conv(x, w) * scale` (channel-wise scaling). Then `out = conv(x,w)*scale + b*scale + shift = (conv(x,w)+b)*scale + shift`. This is mathematically identical to the reference. However, due to floating point associativity, the order of multiplication might differ slightly. In reference, each conv output element is computed as `sum(w*x) + b`, then multiplied by `scale`, then `shift` added. In our approach, `w` is multiplied by `scale` before convolution. Since scaling each weight and then summing is associative in exact arithmetic but not in floating point, there could be a tiny difference. However, the tolerance is 1e-4. For typical random values, the difference between `sum(w_i * x_i) * scale` and `sum((w_i * scale) * x_i)` is in the order of machine epsilon (~1e-7) relative. For a sum of ~100 terms (3x3xC_in, C_in up to 256 => 2304 terms), the rounding error might be a few ulps, so absolute error maybe ~1e-5. That's within 1e-4. So it should pass. But let's test with PyTorch: `conv(x, w*scale) + b*scale + shift` vs `batch_norm(conv(x,w,b), ...)`. For float32, they might differ by ~1e-6. Good. Another nuance: The `bn.running_var` in PyTorch is stored with `unbiased=False`? No, actually I just recalled: PyTorch's `running_var` is indeed the unbiased variance? Wait, I need to be 100% sure. Let's think: In PyTorch 1.x, there was a bug where `running_var` used biased variance and some frameworks used unbiased. The docs say: "The standard-deviation is calculated via the biased estimator, equivalent to torch.var(input, unbiased=False)." This refers to the forward pass. For the running variance, the docs are silent. However, the code in `aten/src/ATen/native/cuda/Normalization.cuh` updates `running_var` using the same `var` as for normalization (biased). So our assumption holds. To be absolutely safe, we could just avoid pre-fusing BN into conv weights and always use `bn_relu_forward` and `bn_relu_maxpool_forward` with `scale` and `shift` computed from running stats. This avoids any potential numerical differences from weight scaling. It also simplifies code because we don't need separate eval/train paths for conv. The only difference is where `scale` and `shift` come from. Let's evaluate the performance of using `_cuda.bn_relu_forward` for eval path as well. In eval path, `scale` and `shift` are constants. The custom kernel reads conv output, applies scale/shift, ReLU, writes result. Memory traffic: conv writes output, kernel reads output, writes ReLU output. That's 1 write + 1 read + 1 write. Reference eval: conv writes output, BN reads output writes BN, ReLU reads BN writes ReLU. That's 1 write + 2 reads + 2 writes. So we save 1 read + 1 write. That's still good. For the second block, using `bn_relu_maxpool_forward` saves even more. If we pre-fuse BN into conv weights for eval, we save the custom kernel for block1 (just `F.relu_`), but conv via cuDNN might be faster than custom conv. Since we still use cuDNN conv, pre-fusing weights just removes the scale/shift from the elementwise kernel. It saves a read of the conv output by the custom kernel because ReLU is in-place. Wait, `F.conv2d(x, w_fused, b_fused)` writes output. `F.relu_(output)` modifies in-place (read-modify-write). Total: 1 write (conv) + 1 read-modify-write (ReLU). Custom `bn_relu_forward` reads conv output and writes ReLU output: 1 read + 1 write. The total memory traffic is the same: both are 2 passes over the data. However, `F.relu_` is an extremely optimized vectorized kernel. Our custom `bn_relu_forward` is also a simple kernel but might not be as optimized as PyTorch's `threshold_` (ReLU). Actually, PyTorch's ReLU kernel is highly optimized with vectorized loads. Our custom kernel also should vectorize if we compile with appropriate flags, but we remain simple. For block 2, pre-fusing BN into conv weights gives us conv output, then `relu_maxpool_forward` custom kernel reads conv output and writes pool output. This is better than `bn_relu_maxpool_forward` because no scale/shift loads. But both are simple. Given the potential numerical risk of weight pre-fusing, and the minimal performance difference, I think it's safer to use the same custom kernels for both train and eval. This also simplifies code: we don't need `_bn_scale_shift` to handle `None`, and we don't need `relu_maxpool_forward`. We can just compute `scale` and `shift` from running stats in eval mode and call `_cuda.bn_relu_forward` and `_cuda.bn_relu_maxpool_forward`. Wait, but `bn_relu_maxpool_forward` needs scale and shift. We have them from running stats. So we can call it. So we can unify the code: ```python for stage in self.feature_extractor: conv1 = stage[0]; bn1 = stage[1] conv2 = stage[3]; bn2 = stage[4] out1 = F.conv2d(x, conv1.weight, conv1.bias, padding=1) scale1, shift1 = self._bn_scale_shift(bn1, out1) x = _cuda.bn_relu_forward(out1, scale1, shift1) out2 = F.conv2d(x, conv2.weight, conv2.bias, padding=1) scale2, shift2 = self._bn_scale_shift(bn2, out2) x = _cuda.bn_relu_maxpool_forward(out2, scale2, shift2) ``` This is the same for train and eval. In eval, `torch.var_mean` is skipped. This avoids any numerical issues from weight pre-fusing and keeps the code simple. But does it perform well in eval? Yes, we still save BN write and ReLU read/write for block1, and BN write + ReLU read/write + MaxPool read for block2. That's significant. The only extra work in eval is computing `scale` and `shift` from running stats (O(C)) and launching the custom kernels. The reference eval would launch BN kernel (which reads running stats) and ReLU kernel. Our custom kernel combines BN+ReLU in one pass. So we should still be faster. Let's go with this unified approach. It's simpler, more numerically stable, and works for both train and eval. But wait: In eval mode, does `nn.BatchNorm2d` use a single kernel for the affine transform? Yes, `batch_norm` in eval mode is a single elementwise kernel. Then ReLU is another. Our custom kernel fuses them. So memory traffic: reference eval does BN (read conv, write bn) + ReLU (read bn, write relu). Our custom does read conv, write relu. That's half the memory traffic for that part. For block2: reference eval does BN (read conv2, write bn2) + ReLU (read bn2, write relu2) + MaxPool (read relu2, write pool). Our custom does read conv2, write pool. That's 1/3 of the memory traffic. So it's definitely faster. Thus, unified path is good and safe. **Let's finalize the code.** `cpp_source` with two kernels. Remove the third kernel. Wait, for block2, we need maxpool after ReLU. Our `bn_relu_maxpool_forward` kernel does exactly that. For block1, we need BN+ReLU. `bn_relu_forward` does that. One more optimization: For `bn_relu_forward`, the output y can overwrite x if we want to save memory. But conv output `out1` is needed only by this kernel. We can do an in-place operation? The kernel reads `x` and writes to `y`. If we pass `y = x` and use `__restrict__`, it might not be safe (read and write same buffer). Better to have a separate output. But we can reuse a buffer. Actually, we can modify `bn_relu_forward` kernel to operate in-place: `y` is same pointer as `x`. But with `__restrict__`, it's undefined behavior if they alias? In CUDA, `__restrict__` means the pointer is not aliased. If we pass the same pointer, the compiler might assume no aliasing and produce wrong code. So we should have separate input and output. However, we can just allocate `y` with `torch.empty_like(x)`. That's what we do. PyTorch's allocator will reuse memory from `out1` because its reference count goes to zero after `bn_relu_forward` returns. So peak memory is similar. Let's also consider if we can improve the kernel by using vectorized loads (float4). But the element count may not be multiple of 4. Also, the indexing is simple; the CUDA compiler might already vectorize. Not necessary. Let's write the code and compile. One more thing: The reference test might call `.eval()` or not. Since we compute scale/shift based on `self.training`, it's robust. Another thing: The `bn_relu_maxpool_forward` kernel computes output size as H/2, W/2 using integer division. If H or W is odd, the kernel handles it by bounds checking. But the host-side `torch::empty({N,C,H/2,W/2}, ...)` also uses integer division. That's consistent. Now, check for any syntax errors in C++. The `bn_relu_maxpool_kernel` uses `int x_idx = ((n * C + c) * H + h_in) * W + w_in;`. That's correct. The `bn_relu_forward` host function: `auto y = torch::empty_like(x);` correct. One more nuance: For `bn_relu_forward`, `scale` and `shift` are 1D tensors. The kernel reads `scale[c]`. We need to make sure `c` is within bounds. Since total elements = N*C*H*W, and `c` computed as `(idx / (H*W)) % C`, it's in [0, C-1]. Let's compile and test. Now, some considerations: - weight2 is accessed per (c_out, dc, kh, kw). In the dc loop, c_out is fixed, dc varies. So weight2[c_out, dc, :, :] is loaded sequentially for dc. They access different rows. row difference is 2 (since 2*oh). Address difference between row r and r+2 is 2*18 = 36. Bank difference is 36 % 32 = 4. So different rows also don't cause conflicts. Wait, actually different rows have different base addresses. But in a warp, threads access different rows simultaneously. Shared memory has 32 banks, and each bank can serve one request per cycle. If two threads in a warp access different rows that map to the same bank, there's a conflict. Row r base address: r * 18. For r=0: 0, r=2: 36, r=4: 72, r=6: 108. Banks of base: 0, 4, 8, 12. Plus column offsets within each row. For column c in row r: bank = (r*18 + c) % 32. Thread (oh=0, ow=0) at row 0, col offset: bank = (0 + col_offset) % 32 Thread (oh=1, ow=0) at row 2, col offset: bank = (36 + col_offset) % 32 = (4 + col_offset) % 32 Thread (oh=2, ow=0) at row 4: bank = (72 + col_offset) % 32 = (8 + col_offset) % 32 Thread (oh=3, ow=0) at row 6: bank = (108 + col_offset) % 32 = (12 + col_offset) % 32 For a fixed col_offset (same kh, kw, dw, and same ow), the banks are offset by 4, 8, 12. No two threads in the warp access the same bank if the offsets are all distinct mod 32. For ow varying 0..7 with fixed oh and kh, kw, dw: col_offset = 2*ow + dw + kw. These are 8 consecutive even or odd numbers. Banks are (base + 2*ow + const) % 32. With step 2, after 16 we repeat. 8 values means no conflicts. So shared memory access should be conflict-free or have minimal conflicts. Good.
